           iv
                                                                                            Introduction


Introduction
Arapahoe County in conjunction with Southeast Metro Stormwater Authority (SEMSWA) is initiating the
Iliff Avenue Corridor Study. The primary goals of this study are to provide recommendations for
improved transportation system mobility, safety, and reliability for commuters and residents along Iliff
Avenue between Quebec Street and Parker Road. The study will also evaluate and recommend water
quality enhancements and stormwater conveyance needs associated with the transportation
improvements recommended in the study.
This Corridor Conditions Report documents the current and anticipated future conditions of the corridor
with regard to the transportation system and environmental resources, as developed from readily
available data and windshield survey information. The information presented in this report will be used
in the development and analysis of improvement alternatives.

Study Area
        The traffic study roadways and the environmental study areas are illustrated in Figure 1. The
        traffic study roadways include Iliff Avenue, from west of the Quebec Street intersection
        (approximately Pontiac Way) to east of the Parker Road intersection (approximately Geneva
        Street). The study area includes the crossing
        roadways immediately north and south of Iliff
        Avenue, capturing the intersection approaches
        to the study corridor.
        Iliff Avenue and the intersecting roadways
        within the project study area lie primarily
        within unincorporated Arapahoe County. Iliff
        Avenue west of Pontiac Way (west of Quebec
        Street) is located within the City and County of
        Denver. The south half of Iliff Avenue from
        Dayton Way to Parker Road is also located
        within the City and County of Denver. East of
        Parker Road, Iliff Avenue is located within the
        City of Aurora.
        The environmental study area is focused around the area of most likely physical impacts of
        corridor transportation improvements. To take into account the potential for indirect or
        secondary effects to community or environmental resources as a result of the proposed action,
        the initial focus area was extended to the back property line of area parcels. The environmental
        study area includes the area generally bounded by Oneida Street to the west, Harvard Avenue
        and the Cherry Creek and High Line Canal Trails to the south, Havana Street to the east, and
        Evans Avenue to the north.

Surrounding Land Use
     The Iliff Avenue study area is located in the unincorporated Four Square Mile Area of Arapahoe
     County in the southeast Denver metropolitan area. The Four Square Mile Area is a suburban
     area surrounded by the City of Aurora and the City and County of Denver. The Four Square Mile
     Sub-Area Plan (2005), part of the Arapahoe County Comprehensive Plan (2001), includes the
     following goals and policies that may apply to the Iliff Avenue corridor (Arapahoe County 2005):
             Limit higher density residential development to arterial (4-lane roads) and major
             collector streets.
             Limit new residential development on local streets recognizing existing densities.
             Limit the intensity of new commercial development to provide services needed at a
             scale in keeping with the surrounding residential uses.
             Re-evaluate the potential for commercial uses along Iliff Avenue and consider the
             constraints of the area for intense commercial uses. Promote mixed-use development
             in non-residential areas.
             Recognize transitional commercial areas and encourage redevelopment or relocation of
             uses that are incompatible with surrounding areas.
             Establish a community identity using public art and signage to set it apart from
             neighboring communities.
             Provide safer streets and increased local street lighting.
             Address the inadequate transportation corridors in the Capital Improvements Program.
             Address drainage issues by implementing the basin plans for the area.
             Improve recreational opportunities by securing sites for parks, playgrounds, shelters.
             Use High Line Canal Trail and Cherry Creek Trail as part of community identity for
             walkable communities and the potential for improved public health.
             Improve the usability and access to the Cherry Creek Trail and the High Line Canal Trail
             from the surrounding neighborhoods by improving pedestrian and bicycle access.

Existing Land Use
     The study area is characterized by a variety of land uses including residential, commercial,
     business, industrial, school, and recreational uses. Land uses along the corridor are illustrated in
     the Existing Multimodal Conditions section of this report.
     West of Quebec Street, land uses consist of multifamily residential developments immediately
     south of Evans Avenue and a mix of retail and industrial uses north of Evans Avenue.
     East of Quebec Street and west of Cherry Creek South Drive and the Cherry Creek Trail, the
     study area consists of retail, industrial, office park and multifamily residential land uses. North
     of Iliff Avenue is the Central Park Center retail area consisting of retail stores, restaurants, and
     automotive services. To the north and east of this area is multifamily housing. Southeast of the
     Quebec Street and Iliff Avenue intersection is a small commercial area that includes automotive
     services and a small retail center. South of Iliff Avenue between Syracuse Way and Cherry
                                                  3
                                                                                               Introduction

      Creek, land uses include a mix of light industrial uses, a self-storage center, the Iliff Business
      Park, and Comcast offices.
      Between Cherry Creek and the High Line Canal, the study area consists of a mixture of industrial,
      retail, residential, and recreational land uses. North of Iliff Avenue, land uses consist primarily
      of industrial uses west of Wabash Street. East of Wabash Street, land uses include a wood
      chipper operation, two small churches, and several single-family homes. East of Yosemite Street
      is a small pocket of multifamily housing.
      South of Iliff Avenue, there is undeveloped land and a small retail center between the Cherry
      Creek Trail and Wabash Street. Between Wabash Street and the High Line Canal is the Cherry
      Creek Country Club with single family homes and a private golf course. The Denver Jewish Day
      School is along Wabash Street, a K-12 school with an annual enrollment of about 370 students.
      East of the High Line Canal, the study area consists primarily of residential developments along
      with some retail and office space surrounding the Parker Road and Iliff Avenue intersection.
      North of Iliff Avenue, single family homes are found between the High Line Canal and Dayton
      Street. Between Dayton Street and Parker Road is the recently-developed Lugano Cherry Creek
      Apartments and a small retail center. East of Parker Road is primarily retail establishments.
      South of Iliff Avenue between the High Line Canal and Parker Road is almost exclusively
      multifamily residential uses. Immediately southwest of the Parker Road intersection is
      office/business space. East of Parker Road is primarily retail stores with a few restaurants.

Future Land Use
      Households and employment along the Iliff Avenue corridor is projected to increase at a modest
      rate from year 2010 to year 2035. Table 1 shows population, household and employment
      estimates and forecasts for 2010 and 2035 within the study area. These estimates are from the
      Denver Regional Council of Government's (DRCOG's) travel demand model, which includes
      existing land uses (based on U.S. Census data) and forecasted land uses based on community
      plans and regional growth expectations.
      As shown, population and household growth is expected to increase within the study area by
      just over 1% annually from 2010 to 2035. Employment is projected to increase by only 0.3%
      annually within the study area.
Table 1: Population, Households and Employment: 2010-2035 within Study Area

Regional Planning Context
     Iliff Avenue within the study area serves as a principal east-west commuter route connecting
     southeast Denver and southern Aurora and Arapahoe County. The roadway is a vital part of the
     regional transportation system as a whole. In order to fully understand the implications of this
     study, potential improvements to the Iliff Avenue corridor are considered in conjunction with
     improvements to the regional transportation system beyond the study area.
     Relevant planning studies from communities within the surrounding region were reviewed to
     identify planned improvements to the transportation system within or in close proximity to the
     Iliff Avenue study area. The planning studies of significance included:
             Arapahoe County 2035 Transportation Plan (2010)
             2035 Metro Vision Regional Transportation Plan (2035 MVRTP) (2013)
             City and County of Denver East Side Mobility Study (2010)
             City of Aurora Comprehensive Plan (2009)
             Arapahoe County Comprehensive Plan (2001)
             Four Square Mile Sub-Area Plan (2005)
     The Arapahoe County 2035 Transportation Plan was updated in 2010. The previous county
     transportation plan completed in 2002 recommended widening Iliff Avenue between Quebec
     Street and Parker Road from four to six lanes. That recommendation was based on travel
     forecasts for 2020. The 2035 Transportation Plan instead recommended a corridor study be
     conducted to fully investigate reasonable alternatives for the corridor. The potential roadway
     and pedestrian/bicycle improvements will be identified by this corridor study process. The 2035
     plan also identifies Quebec Street and Parker Road north of Iliff Avenue as Intelligent
     Transportation System (ITS) Corridors, and Quebec Way/Yosemite Street north of Iliff Avenue
     are recommended for pedestrian and bicycle improvements (Arapahoe County 2010).
     The 2035 Metro Vision Regional Transportation Plan (DRCOG 2013) outlines transportation
     system needs and identifies improvements that can be reasonably expected in the region over
     the next 25 years, given current funding levels. There are no fiscally-constrained roadway
     improvements included in the plan within the Iliff Avenue study area. There are only two
     projects identified in that plan that are within two miles of the study area:
             Hampden Avenue widening from Dayton Street to Havana Street
             Evans Avenue widening from Colorado Boulevard to I-25
     In 2010, the City and County of Denver completed the East Side Mobility Study, which
     recommended reconfiguring Quebec Street between Alameda Avenue and Leetsdale Drive. The
     study recommends widening that section of Quebec Street from a two-lane facility to a three-
     lane facility with one southbound lane and two northbound lanes. The inside northbound lane
     would be an optional left-through lane (City and County of Denver 2010).
     The City of Aurora Comprehensive Plan includes vision and plans/programs for transportation
     within the city. The plan does not identify any improvements along Iliff Avenue, Parker Road, or
     other roadways within close proximity to the Iliff Avenue study area (City of Aurora 2009).

                                                5
                                                                                           Introduction

     The Arapahoe County Comprehensive Plan includes goals, policies and strategies for
     transportation in the County. The plan's transportation goals include promoting an efficient and
     balanced transportation system and coordinating transportation improvements with land use
     development. However, the plan does not identify specific transportation improvements.
     The Four Square Mile Sub-Area Plan was adopted in 2005 as part of the Arapahoe County
     Comprehensive Plan. The transportation goals include addressing the inadequate
     transportation corridors to improve traffic circulation, provide safer streets, and improve
     pedestrian and bicycle access to the trail system. The plan does not identify specific
     transportation improvements along the Iliff Avenue corridor.

Other Area
      Area Projects
     There are improvement efforts taking place in the near term within the vicinity of the study
     area. The projects are primarily focused on maintenance of existing transportation and
     stormwater infrastructure. Each of the committed projects in the area is illustrated in Figure 2.
             Yale Avenue Corridor Traffic Study ­ The City and County of Denver, in conjunction with
             Arapahoe County, is developing a plan for future roadway needs along Yale Avenue
             from Fairfax Street to Syracuse Way. The analysis should be complete by fall of 2013.
             Sidewalk Construction Rosemary Street to Trenton Way ­ Arapahoe County will install
             new sidewalk along the south side of Iliff Avenue between Rosemary Street and Trenton
             Way. This will be done with the completion of the storm water improvements in the
             area, expected in the fall of 2013.
             Wabash Street Pavement Preservation ­ Arapahoe County will perform crack seal, fog
             seal and overlays at various locations. This work is expected in early August 2013.
             Iliff Avenue Pavement Overlay Rehabilitation ­ Arapahoe County will mill and overlay a
             portion of Iliff Avenue pavement over the High Line Canal to address any settlement
             since completion of the underpass. Work is anticipated to begin in early August 2013.
             Traffic Signal Communication Project ­ Arapahoe County is planning on installing an
             improved communication network to provide better coordination between County
             operated signals within the corridor during the summer of 2013.
             Quebec Way Trailhead ­ Arapahoe County Open Spaces will install a trailhead parking
             area for users of the High Line Canal Trail system. No date has been established for
             completion of the project.
             Yosemite Street Curb and Gutter/Stormwater Improvements ­ In late 2013, Arapahoe
             County intends to install curb, gutter, and storm drainage improvements on the west
             side of Yosemite Street between Iliff Avenue and Evans Avenue.
             Parker Road Pavement Overlay ­ The Colorado Department of Transportation (CDOT)
             will mill and overlay Parker Road between Mississippi Avenue and Jewel Avenue. This
             project work has already begun.
             Iliff Avenue/Syracuse Way Storm Sewer Improvements ­ SEMSWA will retrofit the
             existing above ground storm system to an underground system. This work is expected
             to be completed during the summer of 2013.

                                                                                    Corridor Conditions



Corridor Conditions
    Iliff Avenue is a major east-west arterial with four to six through lanes extending from the City of
    Aurora on the east, through the corridor project area, and through the City and County of
    Denver on the west as Evans Avenue. West of the study corridor to Dahlia Street just east of the
    I-25 interchange, Evans Avenue has a four-lane cross-section with relatively narrow lanes and a
    striped two-way left turn lane and numerous access points. East of Parker Road, Iliff Avenue has
    a short stretch of six lanes to the Havana Street intersection and it is four lanes between Havana
    Street and Peoria Street. Iliff Avenue is six lanes with a raised median between Peoria Street
    (approximately one mile east of the study area) and the I-225 interchange. Within the study
    area, the corridor is primarily four travel lanes with raised median, though a painted median
    divides the roadway from Parker Road to Dayton Way.
    Arapahoe County classifies Iliff Avenue as an Urban Arterial roadway. The functional
    classification of Iliff Avenue is described as a Principal Arterial by DRCOG. The speed limit along
    Iliff Avenue is 40 miles per hour (MPH) from east of Parker Road to Quebec Street. West of
    Quebec Street the speed limit decreases to 35 MPH.
    Figure 3 illustrates the corridor intersection's control and lane configurations. There are eight
    signalized intersections along Iliff Avenue within the study area. The Parker Road signal is
    operated by the City of Aurora with a cycle length of 120 seconds during the AM and PM peak
    periods and a non-peak cycle length of 110 seconds. The Dayton Way and Quebec Street signals
    are operated by the City and County of Denver with cycle lengths matching the Parker Road
    signal. All other signals are operated by Arapahoe County with a cycle length of 110 seconds
    during the AM peak period, 100 seconds during the PM peak period, and 90 seconds during non-
    peak periods.
    The unsignalized intersections and major accesses along the corridor operate with various
    movement restrictions. Two of the intersections operate with full movement, two operate with
    restricted right-in/right-out (RI/RO) turning movements, and one operates as a three-quarter
    turn intersection (left-turns allowed from Iliff onto
    the cross-street plus RI/RO). The access drives for
    the Windstream and Lugano at Cherry Creek
    condominium developments operate with
    movement restrictions on the east end of the
    corridor. The unsignalized intersections and major
    accesses with Iliff Avenue include:
             Syracuse Way (RI/RO)
             Cherry Creek South Drive (3/4 movement)
             Emporia Street (full movement)
             Yosemite Circle (RI/RO)
             Windstream Condominiums access (RI/RO)
             Dayton Street (Huntington Estates access) (full movement)
             Lugano at Cherry Creek Condo/Townhome access (3/4 movement)

                                                                                    Corridor Conditions


Surrounding Roadways
             Parker Road (State Highway (SH) 83) ­ Parker Road is state highway facility that serves
             as a principal arterial within the study area. From I-225 south of the study area, Parker
             Road extends north as a six-lane facility and transitions to two lanes northbound and
             three lanes southbound at Havana Street. North of Iliff Avenue, it is a four-lane facility.
             North of the study area, the roadway becomes Leetsdale Drive and continues
             northwest, ending at Colorado Boulevard in the City and County of Denver. Parker Road
             serves both local traffic throughout the Denver metropolitan area and commuter traffic
             destined for/originating from southern suburbs.
             Quebec Street ­ Quebec Street is a minor north-south arterial within the Denver
             metropolitan area. North of I-225, Tamarac Drive extends north to Hampden Avenue
             where it transitions into Quebec Street. It continues north past the study area, through
             much of eastern Denver, across I-70 and I-270 before transitioning into Quebec Parkway
             in Commerce City. Quebec Street is generally a 4-lane minor arterial, though it tapers
             down to 2- and 3-lane sections north of Leetsdale Drive. The roadway has high
             accessibility and serves local and commuter traffic.
             Yosemite Street ­ North of Iliff Avenue, Yosemite Street is a two-lane roadway
             providing access to several residential neighborhoods. The roadway transitions into
             Quebec Way as it extends north and serves as a connection to Florida Avenue. South of
             Iliff Avenue, Yosemite Street enters the gated community of Cherry Creek Country Club.
             Wabash Street ­ Wabash Street is a two-lane roadway providing access to industrial
             land uses north of Iliff Avenue and commercial and residential land uses to the south,
             including the Denver Jewish Day School. A newly constructed crossing of Cherry Creek
             opened in 2011 to connect Wabash Street to Yale Avenue, providing improved
             accessibility, connectivity, and emergency response times. Wabash Street ends
             approximately one-half mile north of Iliff Avenue.
             Cherry Creek South Drive ­ North of Iliff Avenue, Cherry Creek South Drive is a two-lane
             north-south facility that turns to the northwest, terminating at Monaco Parkway. The
             roadway begins again at Holly Street heading northwest before terminating at
             University Boulevard. The roadway provides access to several commercial and industrial
             properties between Iliff Avenue and Quebec Street.

Roadway Features
     Field visits of the study corridor were conducted in March 2013 to document the conditions of
     existing relevant roadway features, such as median treatments, curb and gutter, fences and
     walls, lighting, and observed design deficiencies. The existing roadway features collected along
     the corridor are illustrated in Figure 3.
     Iliff Avenue between Quebec Street and Parker Road has two travel lanes in each direction with
     curb and gutter. There are right turn lanes provided at all of the signalized and unsignalized
     roadway intersections except at Syracuse Way and Dayton Way in the eastbound direction and
     at Cherry Creek South Drive, Valentia Street, and Wabash Street in the westbound direction.

                                                 10
                                                                                      Corridor Conditions

      Continuous acceleration/deceleration lanes are provided in the eastbound direction between
      Wabash Street and the country club maintenance access, in the eastbound direction between
      Dayton Way and Parker Road, and in the westbound direction between Emporia Street and
      Dayton Street.
      The right-of-way along Iliff Avenue is fairly consistent with approximately 80 to 100 feet width
      along the majority of the corridor. This existing right-of-way is less than the Arapahoe County
      standard of 114 feet for a four-lane Urban Arterial. There is attached and detached sidewalk
      provided along the corridor with some missing segments of sidewalk within the study area. The
      existing sidewalk conditions are described in the Pedestrian and Bicycle section of this report.
      Iliff Avenue has portions of raised and painted median. There is raised median of varied width
      with mountable curb and gutter between Quebec Street and Dayton Way with openings at the
      roadway intersections. The median limits turn movements at the unsignalized intersections of
      Syracuse Way and Cherry Creek Drive South, as well as at several residential and commercial
      drives along the corridor. Breaks in the median are provided for driveways at the small retail
      development on the southwest corner of the Wabash Street intersection, at properties on the
                                          north side of Iliff Avenue halfway between Wabash Street and
                                          Yosemite Street, at the golf course maintenance access west
                                          of Dayton Street, and at Lugano at Cherry Creek condominium
                                          developments west of Dayton Way. Between Dayton Way
                                          and Parker Road, there is a double yellow center line with no
                                          raised or painted median between eastbound and westbound
                                          lanes. West of the study area, Evans Avenue is divided with a
                                          painted median. East of Parker Road, Iliff Avenue is divided
                                          with a painted median or continuous median turn lanes.
                                          There is guardrail along Iliff Avenue at the Cherry Creek and
                                          High Line Canal crossings to serve as bridge railing and to
                                          protect against steep slopes.

Infrastructure Conditions
      Arapahoe County maintains a database for County roadway pavement conditions. In 2013,
      most of Iliff Avenue is rated in Good or Fair condition with the east end of the corridor in Fair
      condition. The pavement conditions of the streets intersecting Iliff Avenue are rated in Good
      condition. The section of Wabash Street south of Iliff Avenue, recently constructed with the
      Cherry Creek bridge crossing, is rated with Excellent pavement condition.
      The existing Iliff Avenue over Cherry Creek structure (Str. No. ARA 20-8.5A) was built in 1981 and
      received a sufficiency rating of 90.7 in 2011. The Structure Inspection and Inventory Report
      completed in 2011 stated that the structural capacity of the bridge is in good condition. Routine
      maintenance items include bridge drain cleaning, deck asphalt crack sealing and tree
      trimming. The recommend repairs for safety and aesthetics were to install adequate approach
      rails and re-apply the structural concrete coating.
      The traffic signals at Quebec Street and Dayton Way (maintained by City and County of Denver),
      Trenton Way (maintained by Arapahoe County), and Parker Road (maintained by City of Aurora)

                                                  11
                                                                                              Corridor Conditions

         have mast arm poles. The remaining four traffic signals along the study corridor, maintained by
         Arapahoe County, consist of span wire signal designs.
         The traffic signals along the corridor maintained by City and County of Denver and City of Aurora
         consist of equipment meeting current design standards. Arapahoe County represents the
         condition of traffic signal equipment maintained by the County through a health index rating.
         The measure accounts for numerous considerations with respect to the physical condition and
         functionality of a traffic signal on a scale of 0 to 100. The ratings for the poles and signal heads
         of the traffic signals along Iliff Avenue maintained by Arapahoe County are shown in Table 2.
         The Trenton Way traffic signal poles and signal heads are in relatively poor condition. The other
         traffic signals are in average to above average condition with overall ratings of 89 or above.
         However, poles rated Below Average require monitoring.

         Other areas of potential maintenance issues were noted along the study corridor. There is curb
         and gutter damage along the south side of Iliff Avenue between Syracuse Way and Trenton Way
         and along the north side of Iliff Avenue between Emporia Street and Parker Road. There is also
         substantial cracking in the median cover material along the entire corridor with vegetation
         growing through the wide cracks.

Fences
Fences and Walls
         There are sections of fences and/or walls along Iliff Avenue between Quebec Street and Parker
         Road. All fences and walls are located behind the sidewalk or curb and gutter and are offset
         from the roadway. No fences or walls create issues with clear zone on the project. The wood

        fence on the south side of Iliff Avenue from the High Line Canal to Dayton Way impacts the sight
        distance for northbound right turns at the Dayton Way intersection. A summary of the fences
        and walls along the study corridor is provided in Table 3.

Roadway Lighting
        Lighting along the Iliff Avenue corridor is limited to street lights on traffic signal poles and a
        short segment of continuous roadway lighting on the east end of the corridor. There are street
        lights on the signal poles at Quebec Street, Dayton Way, and Parker Road. There are also street
        lights located on the south side of Iliff Avenue between Dayton Way and Parker Road.

Roadway Deficiencies
        The corridor was reviewed for potential deficiencies related to the operations and maintenance
        of the study corridor from Quebec Street to Parker Road, including clear zone/obstructions, side
        slope (i.e., too steep without guardrail), sight distance (vertical and horizontal), lane taper
        lengths, and sidewalk gaps. Potential deficiencies are listed in Table 4 and locations identified in
        Figure 3. The existing sidewalk conditions are described in the Pedestrian and Bicycle section of
        this report.
        Left and right turn acceleration and deceleration lanes exist at multiple intersections along the
        corridor. The length of the existing acceleration and deceleration was compared to the
        requirements of the Arapahoe County Infrastructure Design and Construction Standards. The
        locations that do not meet the standard length are noted in the table of Potential Roadway
        Deficiencies.
        There are intersection approaches along the corridor that do not have acceleration or
        deceleration lanes, but would warrant lanes based on Arapahoe County Roadway Design and
        Technical Criteria. Turn volumes warrant right turn deceleration lanes on westbound Iliff
        Avenue at Cherry Creek South Drive and eastbound Iliff Avenue at Dayton Way. Future 2035
        forecasted turn volumes warrant right turn acceleration lanes on westbound Iliff Avenue from
        Wabash Street and from Yosemite Street and on eastbound Iliff Avenue from Quebec Street.

Stormwater Facilities
           Facilities
         The drainage outlet along Iliff Avenue is Cherry Creek with a small area on the west study limits
         outleting first to the north along Quebec Street. The existing storm sewer along Iliff Avenue
         from Parker Road to Cherry Creek dates back to 1981. From the High Line Canal crossing to the
         outfall at Cherry Creek, the storm sewer is a 60-inch diameter reinforced concrete pipe (RCP).
         This storm sewer system captures runoff from Iliff Avenue and adjacent areas immediately
         north and south of the roadway. This drainage system conveys flows from the Huntington
         Outfall and flows that are from east of Parker Road. Outside of this area, the storm flows are
         managed by storm sewers that outlet separately to Cherry Creek outside of the study area.
Figure 4. Existing Storm Sewer along Iliff Avenue




         The potential use of the High Line Canal for stormwater quality treatment and water quality is
         being studied by the Urban Drainage and Flood Control District (UDFCD) in cooperation with
         surrounding local agencies. There is a small storm system from Huntington Estates that outfalls
         into the High Line Canal, causing erosion at the outfall.


                                                      14
                                                                                     Corridor Conditions

        At the west end of the study area, surface runoff along Iliff Avenue from Quebec Street to
        Syracuse Way are collected in a storm sewer that outlets north of Iliff Avenue. A small area west
        of Syracuse Way appears to flow under the High Line Canal to the existing Iliff Avenue storm
        sewer. The flows from west of the High Line Canal and the Iliff Avenue flows are transported
        alternately by open channel and storm sewer easterly to Cherry Creek. The Four Square Mile
        Outfall System Plan (OSP) suggested a 42-inch diameter RCP for this storm sewer sizing.
        SEMSWA and other sources note that the outfall at Cherry Creek is a 24-inch diameter RCP.

Vehicular Traffic Operations
Due to vehicular interactions between closely-spaced intersections, the capacity and operations of an
urban arterial corridor, such as Iliff Avenue, is typically defined by the operations of the intersections.
Intersection operational analyses were completed for the Iliff Corridor Study utilizing methods outlined
in the latest Highway Capacity Manual (HCM 2010) using Synchro (Version 8, build 804, revision 790)
traffic analysis software (TRB 2010). The existing intersection and corridor lane configurations and
balanced peak hour traffic volumes for existing and the horizon year were used to analyze the Levels of
Service (LOS) and control delay at each study intersection for the AM and PM peak hours for each of the
analysis years. Delays associated with bus stops/movements and pedestrians that currently occur along
the corridor were accounted for in the analysis.
LOS is directly related to control delay and is a measure of traffic flow and level of congestion at an
intersection measured on a scale of A to F. LOS A describes conditions with essentially uninterrupted
flow and minimal delay. LOS F describes a breakdown of traffic flow where there exists excessive
congestion delay. Signalized capacity results in an overall LOS representative of all movements through
the intersection. Unsignalized capacity analysis produces LOS results for each vehicle movement that
yields the right-of-way to conflicting traffic. Table 7 summarizes the signalized and unsignalized LOS
thresholds used in this analysis.
Table 7. LOS Criteria
                                               LOS CRITERIA
                                 SIGNALIZED DELAY     TWO WAY STOP CONTROL
                                            1                           2
                          LOS        RANGE                  DELAY RANGE
                                         (SEC)                    (SEC)


                         A       0-10                  0-10
                         B       10-20                 10-15
                         C       20-35                 15-25
                         D       35-55                 25-35
                         E       55-80                 35-50
                         F       80 and above          50 and above
                         1
                          HCM 2010 Exhibit 18-4, page 18-6
                         2
                          HCM 2010 Exhibit 19-1, page 19-2

     along Iliff Avenue between Quebec Street and
     Parker Road fall within 31,000 and 34,000 vpd
     with the greatest volumes east of Cherry Creek
     South Drive. East of Parker Road, Iliff Avenue
     experiences daily traffic volumes of
     approximately 27,650 vehicles.

       The daily counts were collected with hourly breakdowns of volumes at each location. Figure 8
       shows the hourly fluctuations of the daily counts collected at two locations along Iliff Avenue;
       east of Quebec Street and east of Yosemite Street. At both locations, the traffic is characterized
       by distinct peak hour directionality with heavy traffic flow in the westbound direction in the AM
       peak commute hours and in the eastbound direction in the PM peak commute hours. This
       traffic pattern is a reflection of the high use of the corridor by commuters living in the southeast
       Denver metropolitan area destined for Colorado Boulevard, Cherry Creek, downtown Denver
       and the I-25 corridor.

      In order to analyze and simulate traffic operations in the corridor with Synchro traffic analysis
      software, a network of AM and PM peak hour traffic with volumes balanced between major
      intersections was created. The counts collected for this study were utilized in conjunction with
      other available traffic volume data to balance peak hour traffic volumes through the corridor.

Existing Operational Analysis
      The existing intersection operations are illustrated in Figure 7 and summarized in Table 8. As
      shown, the Quebec Street and Parker Road signalized intersections along Iliff Avenue operate at
      LOS E or worse during the AM and/or PM peak hours. All other signalized intersections within
      the study area operate at LOS D or better during each of the peak hours. Three of the four
      unsignalized intersections along Iliff Avenue within the study area have traffic movements that
      were reported to operate at LOS E or worse during the AM and/or PM peak hours. The poorly
      performing unsignalized intersections are Cherry Creek South Drive, Dayton Street, and Emporia
      Street. Existing intersection operation reports are included in Appendix C.
      In addition to intersection LOS, queues along Iliff Avenue were reviewed to indicate areas of
      congestion. There are relatively long intersection vehicle queues throughout the corridor with
      the queues at the Quebec Street, Rosemary Street, Wabash Street, and Dayton Way signals
      extending beyond the upstream intersection during the AM and/or PM peak hour.
      Queuing problems generally occur in the westbound direction during the AM peak hour and in
      the eastbound direction during the PM peak hour, consistent with the peak hour commuting
      travel direction of the corridor.

Future Traffic Conditions
        The horizon year for this study is 2035, consistent with the horizon year for the current adopted
        DRCOG Regional Transportation Plan. The DRCOG 2035 regional travel demand model was used
        to develop 2035 traffic forecasts for the study area roadways. Due to the complexity of real-
        world travel behavior, the DRCOG travel demand model is not expected to provide precise
        traffic volume forecasts. To improve the reliability of forecasts, a post-processing adjustment of
        the 2035 traffic volumes was performed. The adjustment methodology compared the existing
        year model traffic volumes to actual traffic counts in the study area. The 2035 traffic forecasts
        were adjusted based on this factor for model versus actual traffic volumes.

Travel Demand Model
        The DRCOG model was reviewed and modifications made to improve the model's
        representation of actual land uses and roadway and transit systems. As a regional model, the
        travel characteristics within the study area are affected by elements of the model well outside
        the study area. In order to provide reliable traffic forecasts for the study, model modifications
        were made both within the study area and a larger area surrounding the study area. The model
        was generally reviewed between Dahlia Street and Peoria Street and between Leetsdale
        Drive/Mississippi Avenue and Hampden Avenue. Adjustments were made to the roadway
        network, the traffic analysis zone (TAZ) structure, and socioeconomic data.

Roadway and Transit Network
        The roadway and transit networks in the DRCOG 2010 and 2035 models were checked for
        accuracy within the vicinity of the project. Roadway links were reviewed for accurate facility
        types, connections, and laneages. Transit networks were reviewed for accurate route alignment
        and connections.

                                                    23
                                                                              Vehicular Traffic Operations

        No changes were necessary for the 2010 and 2035 transit networks. Only minor changes to the
        2010 and 2035 roadway networks were required, including removing the non-existent Oneida
        Street bridge connection across Cherry Creek, adding Iowa Avenue, Dayton Way/Yale Avenue,
        and Wabash Street (including the new crossing of Cherry Creek at Yale Avenue). TAZ
        connections were also improved where appropriate.

TAZ Structure
        The DRCOG regional travel demand model utilizes TAZs to represent land uses throughout the
        region. Socioeconomic data is aggregated and assigned to the TAZ zone system. Within and
        surrounding the study area, numerous zones in the TAZ system cover large and occasionally
        disjointed areas. For the purposes of this study, the TAZs were modified in order to improve the
        distribution of employment and households and the access points to the roadway system within
        the 2010 and 2035 models.
        Eleven TAZs were split and their land uses disaggregated into 30 zones, resulting in an additional
        19 zones within the vicinity of the project study area. The modified TAZ system is illustrated in
        Figure 9.

Socioeconomic Data
        The DRCOG 2010 and 2035 socioeconomic data were also reviewed for accuracy for zones inside
        and within close proximity to the study area, including portions of unincorporated Arapahoe
        County, the City of Aurora, and the City and County of Denver.
        Household and employment numbers from the 2010 model were compared to 2010 Census and
        Local Employment Dynamics data and adjustments were made where appropriate (U.S. Census
        Bureau 2010). The 2010 land use adjustments were applied, in varying degrees, to the 2035
        socioeconomic data, in order to preserve the growth in households and employment projected
        by DRCOG.
        The adjustments to land uses within the vicinity of the study area resulted in an increase in
        households of approximately 185 and 10 in the 2010 and 2035 models, respectively.
        Employment totals were adjusted by approximately 2,470 and 2,600 in the 2010 and 2035
        models, respectively. This equates to an increase of just over 10% in both models.

2035 Traffic Conditions
      Projected traffic forecasts for 2035 within the study area are illustrated in Figure 10, along with
      the intersection LOS. Traffic along Iliff Avenue from east of Parker Road to west of Quebec
      Street increases between approximately 2,300 and 7,600 daily vehicles by 2035. This equates to
      less than a 1% annual increase in daily traffic volumes throughout the corridor. Parker Road
      experiences an increase of 0.7% and 0.9% annual growth to the north and south of Iliff Avenue,
      respectively. Quebec Street experiences the greatest increase in traffic in the area with an
      annual growth of 1.9% to the north and south of Iliff Avenue. Traffic along cross-streets
      between Parker Road and Quebec Street are generally projected to increase by no more than
      1% annually. Only Wabash Street south of Iliff Avenue is projected to experience more than 1%
      annual growth in traffic at 1.2%.
      Parallel facilities to Iliff Avenue to the north and south of the study area are projected to
      experience low to moderate annual growth rates. Mississippi Avenue, a 4- to 6-lane major
      arterial to the north, is projected to experience 0.8% annual growth east of Parker Road.
      Hampden Avenue, a 6-lane major arterial to the south, is projected to experience 1.4% annual
      growth. The higher growth rate along Hampden Avenue may be partly due to planned widening
      of the roadway from Dayton Street to Havana Street.
      The low to moderate traffic growth projections within the study area and the surrounding area
      are typical of developed areas with limited development opportunities and minimal planned
      capacity improvements to regional roadway facilities. Additionally, the high congestion along
      roadways west and north of the study area may contribute to future trips avoiding the area.
      An evaluation of vehicular origin and destination patterns was conducted by performing a
      "select link" or "critical link" analysis using the DRCOG regional travel demand model. A select
      link analysis is a modeling tool that isolates a specific roadway segment and provides trip origin
      and destination data for that segment. For the purposes of this study, the segment of Iliff
      Avenue between Wabash Street and Yosemite Street was selected for this analysis. Select link
      model runs were performed using both the 2010 and 2035 travel demand models. Figure 11
      illustrates the results of a select link analysis. The analysis shows that of all trips that traveled
      along this segment of Iliff Avenue, approximately 72% originated from and were destined for
      areas outside the study area in 2010. In 2035, this percentage is projected to increase by 2% to
      74%. Trips of this type are often associated with commuter trips.
      The 2035 peak hour traffic operations are summarized in Table 10 along with results of the
      existing operational analysis for comparison. The intersection operation reports are included in
      Appendix C.
      As shown, four of the eight signalized intersections along the Iliff Avenue study corridor are
      projected to operate at LOS E or worse during the 2035 AM and/or PM peak hours assuming no
      improvements to the existing corridor. The intersections with reported LOS E or worse
      operations are Quebec Street, Wabash Street, Dayton Way, and Parker Road. All other
      signalized intersections were reported to operate at LOS C or better. The County's acceptable
      LOS standard for intersections in urban areas is LOS D.

       the existing peak hour conditions, queuing problems are expected to generally occur in the
       westbound direction during the AM peak hour and in the eastbound direction during the PM
       peak hour, consistent with the peak hour commuting travel direction of the corridor.




                                                  30
                                                                                                                                                 Crash History


Crash History
Crash data along the Iliff Avenue corridor study area (from west of Quebec Street to east of Parker
Road) were compiled and reviewed for a three-year period including 2008, 2010, and 2012. Agency data
provided for 2009 and 2011 were incomplete. Data in the corridor was provided by Arapahoe County,
CDOT, and the City and County of Denver. The vast majority of the crashes in the corridor occurred at
the intersections, and most of those that were not directly at an intersection were of a type that was
likely related to congestion extending from an intersection. For this reason, all corridor crash data has
been summarized by intersection in the three-year crash summary illustrated in Figure 12.

Traffic Crash Types and Location
        The type of crashes (rear end, broadside, head-on, sideswipe, etc.) at an intersection is an
        important consideration because it not only relates to the severity of the crashes, but also
        relates to the potential corrective measures that may be developed. The types of crashes at
        each intersection are summarized in Table 11. Rear end crashes are by far the most common,
        accounting for over 55% of all crashes in the corridor.

Intersection Crash Rates
          Crash rates (per million entering vehicles ­ MEV) were calculated at each intersection to
          compare the crash frequency between intersections while also considering the amount of traffic
          that passes through each intersection. Crashes per MEV are summarized in Table 12. The
          Parker Road intersection had the highest crash rate, followed by the Quebec Street intersection.
          For comparison, average crash rates (per MEV) from a few state departments of transportation
          were reviewed and summarized as a benchmark (note that CDOT does not calculate intersection
          crash rates). The resultant average rate developed for signalized arterial intersections was 0.64
          crashes per MEV, and the average rate developed for unsignalized arterial intersections was
          0.41. Based on these comparable average crash rates, the Quebec Street, Dayton Way, and
          Parker Road intersection crash rates were above average with the rate at Parker Road twice the
          average rate. All other intersection rates were below these informal average intersection rates.
          Intersection crashes in the corridor were also compared to CDOT's Safety Performance
          Functions (SPFs)1. In this approach, the number of annual crashes at each intersection was
          compared to CDOT's SPF for urban arterial intersections. The SPFs incorporate crash data, daily
          traffic volume, and roadway characteristics (lanes, signalization, number of approaches) to
          develop an expected range of annual traffic crash frequency. Using this method, all intersections
          in the Iliff corridor are below the expected crash frequency predicted by CDOT's SPFs. Even the



1
  Safety Performance Functions for Intersections, Colorado Department of Transportation, DTD Applied Research and Innovation Branch, Report
No. CDOT-2009-10 Final Report, December 2009
                                                                   33
                                                                                                               Crash History

             high volume intersections at the ends of the corridor (at Quebec Street and Parker Road) are
             within the range of accidents predicted by the SPFs.

Severity Index
             Another method of reviewing and comparing traffic crash data is to incorporate the crash
             severity information at each intersection and develop weighted rates based on severity.
             Agencies, including Arapahoe County, often weight fatal crashes by a factor of 12, injury crashes
             by a factor of 5, and PDO crashes by a factor of 1. Applying these severity weights to the Iliff
             corridor yields the Severity Index Adjustment detailed in Table 13 for each intersection. Using
             this method, the Parker Road intersection had the most severe score (121) with the Quebec
             Street intersection a close second (116).
             The crash totals adjusted for severity were then used to recalculate the intersection crash rate
             (using the MEV data), yielding crashes per MEV with Severity Index Adjustment. Reviewing
             these rates shows that the Trenton Way and Dayton Way intersections yielded high crash
             severity rates along with the Parker Road and Quebec Street intersections.

             By using the length of the corridor, the daily traffic volume, and the total number of crashes that
             occurred during the three years that were reviewed, a crash rate per million vehicle miles of
             travel (MVM) was calculated for the corridor as a whole. It was determined that this corridor
             has an approximate crash rate of 3.66 crashes per MVM. This was then compared to CDOT
             statewide average traffic crash rates2 for comparable roadway types as follows:

     The Iliff Avenue corridor has a crash rate substantially higher than CDOT's principal arterial
     average crash rate.

Public Input on Corridor Crash History
     Members of the public who attended the initial public meeting for the study also provided input
     on the crash history in the Iliff Avenue corridor. One comment often heard was that there are
     numerous crashes in the winter when roadway conditions are slick on the hill in the vicinity of
     Dayton Street and Parker Road in the eastern end of the study area. A subsequent review of the
     crash reports in this area did not show a concentration of ice and snow related accidents.
     One possible explanation is that many "fender benders" that occur on winter days are during
     periods when the public is on "accident alert". During these times, the police typically do not
     respond unless there are injuries involved. When there are no injuries and the police do not
     respond, the citizens involved often need to complete "counter reports" themselves. These
     types of reports are sometimes not completed, or sometimes not logged into an agency's traffic
     crash database in the same way as officer-generated reports. This may explain why the ice and
     snow related accidents referenced by the public may not be showing up in this crash review.

                                                                         Corridor Multimodal Mobility


Corridor Multimodal Mobility
    In April 2013, a comprehensive Walking and Bicycling Audit (WB Audit) of the Iliff Avenue
    corridor between Quebec Street and Parker Road was conducted using evaluation tools and
    survey methods that are recommended by CDOT, National Center for Walking and Bicycling
    (bikewalk.org) and the Federal Highway Administration (FHWA). The survey methods provide
    an assessment of the existing conditions pedestrians and bicyclists encounter when traveling in
    the corridor. This includes conditions for those who might have limited mobility, a disability, or
    walk with children. The WB Audit also accounts for bicyclists who are novices, children, or those
    who are riding for non-recreational purposes (commuting, shopping, schools, etc).
    Photos and measurements were collected during peak and off-peak travel times to document
    the existing conditions for all users. Pedestrian and bicycle travel between destinations was
    observed and informal conversations were held with people walking and bicycling in the
    corridor. The WB Audit also documented surrounding land use within a 6-minute walk and 3-
    minute bicycle ride of the corridor. This information provides the basis for potential walking and
    bicycling travel underrepresented in traditional pedestrian counts at intersections.

Potential Non1
          Non1Motorized Travel Demand
    A database of existing land use and population demographics from the Mile High Connects
    project was reviewed to document conditions near the Iliff Avenue corridor within the study
    area. This database includes information on populations that are dependent on walking,
    bicycling, and transit for access to school, jobs, and shopping. It also provides results from
    travel-related survey questions. All of the data is based on the results of the 2010 US Census.
    The database includes the following information:
            Low income households
            Distribution of housing types
            Residents over 55 years of age
            Federally-subsidized housing locations
            Employment types
            Non-automobile commuting
    The data in zones near the Iliff Avenue corridor show the following:
            There are several areas on the east and west end of the study area that are classified as
            low income households that may rely on walking, bicycling, or transit for their primary
            means of travel to school, work and shopping.
            There are concentrations of income restricted housing in the central and east sections
            of the study area within short walking distance of transit stops and the regional trails.
            85% of the people living in the study area are under 55 years of age with multiple family
            members in a household that could have a wide range of trip destinations.



                                                36
                                                                           Corridor Multimodal Mobility

             There are concentrations of federally-subsidized housing in the western section of the
             study area that may rely on walking, bicycling, or transit for their primary means of
             travel to school, work and shopping.
     Figure 13 illustrates the existing residential, employment and commercial areas and the
     proximity between land uses that could generate demand for short walking and bicycling trips.
     An evaluation of the land uses shows that the study area has approximately 7,000 residents and
     3,500 employees within a 6-minute walk or a 3-minute bicycle ride of a retail center, bus route,
     or a regional trail access point. DRCOG travel survey results indicate that residents in the region
     walk, bike and use transit for approximately 8 - 10% of their non-work trips. US Census data
     indicate that approximately 9 - 14% of Arapahoe County residents walk, bike or use transit when
     traveling to work.

Existing Multimodal Conditions
     Iliff Avenue between Quebec Street and Parker Road is served by Regional Transportation
     District (RTD) Route 21 and the 79L. Route 21 provides all-day connections to local destinations
     east and west of the study area, including Colorado Station (light rail lines E, F, & H). The
     current bus travel time to Colorado Station from the bus stop at Iliff Avenue and Parker Road is
     19 minutes. The light rail trip from Colorado Station is an additional 15 minutes northbound to
     downtown Denver or 10 minutes southbound to the Denver Technological Center. Route 79L
     provides limited stop peak hour access from Nine Mile Station in Aurora (light rail line H) to Civic
     Center Station in downtown Denver. Route 79L only serves a few stops in the study corridor
     and only travels from Aurora to downtown Denver. The current bus travel time from Iliff
     Avenue at Yosemite Street to downtown Denver is 37 minutes.
     There are approximately 850 daily transit rides (boarding and alighting) in the study area
     corridor. This ridership is consistent with other arterial
     corridors in the region with daily bus service with 30-
     minute headways, suburban development patterns, free
     parking, and emerging multimodal infrastructure. Bus
     stop details with routes, as well as the existing sidewalk
     and regional trail user counts, are shown in Figure 14.
     Sidewalk widths along the corridor range from 5 to 10
     feet. There are missing sidewalks on the south side of
     the corridor between Rosemary Street and Trenton Way
     and between Cherry Creek Trail and Valentia Street.
     Trail counts for the Cherry Creek and High Line Canal Trails were provided by CDOT. The counts
     include daily use for pedestrians (including runners) and bicyclists from April 2012 to February
     2013. The counts along the Cherry Creek Trail are combined and did not break out pedestrians
     from bicyclists. The average daily user totals for the months between May and September
     (including weekends) were 2,620 users for the Cherry Creek Trail and 970 users for the High Line
     Canal Trail. Since 2009, CDOT has collected trail usage data at eight regional trail locations. Based
     on this data, the daily usage on the Cherry Creek Trail at this location is one of the highest count
     locations while the High Line Canal Trail at Iliff Avenue experiences average usage, consistent with
     the daily volumes found on the C-470 multi-use trail.

Multimodal Mobility Challenges and Opportunities
      Alternative mode travel challenges and opportunities for the Iliff Avenue corridor are
      summarized below by mode. This summary was developed from the existing conditions data,
      walking and biking audit, and input gathered from the general public. These opportunities
      provide a basis for developing design solutions that promote safe travel for all roadway users
      and improve the overall liveability for the corridor.

Pedestrian
              People walk in the Iliff Avenue corridor to many destinations and for many purposes.
              There is a latent demand for walking trips in the study area.
              Sidewalks are present along most of the corridor with varying width from 5 to 10 feet.
              Connections to some adjacent land uses from existing sidewalks are not present.
              Many of the intersection signage, markings, and signal control are not ADA-compliant.
              The condition of most sidewalks is adequate, but maintenance is needed.
              Recurring debris along the sidewalks impedes safe travel for all users.
              The crosswalk markings are not uniform along the corridor.
              There are inoperable or deteriorated pedestrian signals at intersections.
              Mid-block crossings are typically occurring at the west end of the corridor.
              Pedestrian scale lighting is not present in the corridor.
              Roadway and surrounding area drainage presents safety challenges for all users during
              rain storms and after snow storms.
                                                  44
                                                                           Corridor Multimodal Mobility

Bicycle
Bicycle
          There are many bicycle skill types using the corridor for different purposes.
          New residences in the corridor are promoting bicycling and regional trail access.
          Bicyclists are currently riding on Iliff Avenue with vehicles.
          The current pathways in the corridor do not have adequate width, setback, signage,
          striping, or sight distance features based on the guidelines for shared use paths.
          There is a lack of general wayfinding along Iliff Avenue leading bicyclists to the Cherry
          Creek Trail and High Line Trail.
          There are no publicly-provided and secure bicycle parking options in the corridor (racks,
          lockers, storage units) particularly near bus stops.
          The underpass configuration for the Cherry Creek Trail requires the use of the sidewalk
          on the south side of the Iliff Avenue bridge. The width of this sidewalk and the trail
          volume is not consistent with national standards (10-foot recommended minimum for
          shared use paths).

Transit
          The current routes provide all-day connections to the destinations in the corridor.
          There is a frequent connection to the Colorado Station's light rail routes.
          Travel time on Route 21 will increase in the future as traffic volumes increase.
          The current travel time via bus to regional employment centers (from bus stops in the
          corridor) is comparable to vehicular commute time during AM and PM peak hours.
          The current travel time via bus to regional employment centers (from bus stops in the
          corridor) is 50% longer than vehicular commute time during off-peak periods.
          Most of the bus stops do not adequately accommodate 40-foot buses and need
          maintenance for deteriorating curbs and concrete areas.

                                                                                  Environmental Overview



Environmental Overview
The purpose of the environmental overview within this report is to identify environmental resources
early in the planning process in order to consider sensitive environmental resources. The intent is not to
identify impacts but rather to identify potential "red flag" resource areas for use in alternatives analysis
to avoid and minimize impacts to resources. If a recommended improvement project receives state or
federal funding, the results of this study will be carried forward at that time into project development
with additional environmental review (National Environmental Policy Act (NEPA)-level or similar state
environmental review process).
This section identifies environmental resources and environmentally sensitive areas based on readily
available data and field survey information. Data was collected to identify the existing environmental
resources in the area in the spring of 2013 using data resources such as file searches from Arapahoe
County, Denver County, and other agencies; a literature review; field surveys; and windshield surveys.
Natural environmental resources topics summarized in this report are illustrated in Figure 24 and
include:
               Floodways and Floodplain
                Threatened and Endangered Species and Wildlife Habitat
Built environmental and community resources topics summarized in this report are illustrated in Figure
25 and include:
                Parks and Recreation Resources (Section 4(f)/6(f))
                Hazardous Materials
                Cultural Resources (Historic and Archeological Resources)
                Paleontological Resources
                Air Quality
Other environmental and community resources topics summarized in this report include:
                Noise
                Environmental Justice

Floodways and Floodplain
        A review of UDFCD and Federal Emergency Management Agency data was conducted on April 3,
        2013 to identify any potential floodplains in the project area. One 100 year floodplain associated
        with Cherry Creek was identified in the project area as shown on Figure 24.
        Future projects should be developed in a manner that minimizes impacts to the mapped
        floodplain. Any impacts to the floodplain should be evaluated with future design of the project.
        Work within the mapped floodplain will require a floodplain development permit. Changes to
        the FEMA regulatory floodplain may require a formal map revision process.
