         The field review did not observe any raptor nests or other nesting migratory birds in the vicinity
         of the roadway. No other wildlife species were observed during the field review. A bald eagle
         nest was identified in 2006 along Cherry Creek near Monaco Parkway. This nest has been
         inactive since it was first observed in 2006. Swallows may also nest at the Iliff Avenue bridge
         over Cherry Creek. None were observed in 2013, but swallow nest surveys should be completed
         prior to conducting any work at this bridge.
         The Cherry Creek corridor in the vicinity of Iliff Avenue has been block-cleared by the U.S. Fish
         and Wildlife Service for the threatened Preble's meadow jumping mouse (Zapus hudsonius
         preblei). This block clearance has been in effect since 2010. One federal listed plant species in
         Arapahoe County, the threatened Ute ladies' tresses orchid (Spiranthes diluvialis) was also
         block-cleared in the Iliff Study Area. The Colorado butterfly plant (Gaura neomexiana ssp.
         coloradensis) was also blocked cleared in the study area. This threatened plant species is not
         formally listed as occurring in Arapahoe County, but USFWS mapping showed the study area as
         block-cleared for the species. The block clearance for these listed plant species is in effect until

                                                        49
                                                                               Environmental Overview

     May 14, 2014. It is assumed that this block clearance will be re-issued based on the poor habitat
     for these species along Cherry Creek downstream from Cherry Creek Reservoir. In addition, the
     field review conducted on May 16, 2013, observed no suitable habitat for either the Ute ladies
     tresses orchid or the Colorado butterfly plant in the vicinity of the roadway. Therefore, no
     federal candidate, threatened, and endangered species are likely to occur in the study area.

Parks and Recreation Resources (Section 4(f)/6(f))
     Section 4(f) of the Department of Transportation Act of 1966 stipulates that the FHWA and
     other Department of Transportation agencies cannot approve the use of land from publicly
     owned parks, recreational areas, wildlife and waterfowl refuges, or public and private historic
     sites unless there is no feasible and prudent alternative to the use of land, and the action
     includes all possible planning to minimize harm to the property resulting from use. Section 4(f)
     applies to projects that receive federal funding from or require approval by an agency of the
     U.S. Department of Transportation.
     The Land and Water Conservation Fund (LWCF) Act of 1965 established a Federal funding
     program to assist states in developing outdoor recreation sites. Section 6(f) of the act prohibits
     the conversion of property acquired or developed with these funds to a non-recreational
     purpose without the approval of the National Park Service (NPS) (NPS, 2008). The primary
     reason to define impacts to properties protected by 6(f) is to avoid a conversion of these
     properties during the project construction. If a conversion of land cannot be avoided, efforts
     should be made to minimize or mitigate effects to these properties. During NEPA, the lead
     agencies must mitigate any impacts to these properties with a replacement of lands of equal
     value, location and usefulness of the impacted land.
     The Arapahoe County Open Space Master Plan (2010), Arapahoe County Four Square Mile Sub-
     Area Plan (2005), Denver County Cherry Creek Greenway Corridor Master Plan Report (2012),
     and the East Iliff Avenue Underpass Feasibility Study (2009) were consulted in combination with
     a survey of GIS data provided by Arapahoe County and City and County of Denver to identify
     existing and future parks and recreation facilities within the study area. The study area has 0
     acres of parks, 2 multi-use trails, approximately 5.5 acres of land designated as greenway, and
     approximately 3.3 acres of trailhead alongside Cherry Creek (Arapahoe County 2013).
     Planning documents for the City of Aurora, Arapahoe County, and the City and County of Denver
     include the goals of preserving and expanding parkland, open space, and recreation facilities.
     The Arapahoe County Open Space Master Plan (2010) indicates that the Iliff Avenue corridor
     suffers from a shortage of access points to public trails, lack of street connectivity between
     developments, and crossings of Cherry Creek. The Open Space Master Plan states that
     "widening of Iliff Avenue...and continued trail improvements to Cherry Creek and trail
     connections to the High Line Canal will...improve connectivity and continuity within and into the
     Four Square Mile Area". The Four-Square-Mile Subarea Plan also identifies the goals of
     improving trails and neighborhood access to trails, as well as securing additional park sites in
     order to improve opportunities for passive recreation.
     A file search was conducted in March 2013 to determine whether LWCF 6(f) funds were used on
     any facilities within the environmental study area. No facilities were identified.

                                                 50
                                                                                  Environmental Overview

Park and Recreation/Section 4(f) Resources in the Study Area
        There are two multi-use trails and one designated trailhead within the study area. Park and
        recreation resources are depicted in Figure 25 and described below.

High Line Canal Trail
        The High Line Canal Trail is a hard and soft surface multi-use trail that runs north parallel to the
        High Line Canal through Douglas, Arapahoe, Denver, and Adams counties. The High Line Canal is
        owned by Denver Water and the trail is maintained by the jurisdictions in which the trail section
        is located through a license agreement with Denver Water (Denver Water 2013). The
        maintaining jurisdictions include City of Aurora, Cherry Hills Village, City and County of Denver
        Department of Parks and Recreation, Douglas County, Greenwood Village, Highlands Ranch
        Metro District, and South Suburban Parks and Recreation.
        Because of water conservation needs and the canal's inefficiencies, Denver Water has
        discontinued use of the lower 22 miles of the canal for irrigation water distribution. Concerned
        agencies, including Arapahoe County, developed a study to investigate alternatives that would
        preserve the canal as a recreational resource (Denver Water 2013).
        Trail users include pedestrians, cyclists, and horse-back riders, and benches are provided
        intermittently along the canal. In general, the High Line Canal Trail runs along the west side of
        the High Line Canal within the study area, although the trail crosses to the east side of the canal
        via a pedestrian bridge directly south of Iliff Avenue for approximately 400 feet (Iliff Ave
        Underpass). A pedestrian underpass was recently constructed under Iliff Avenue so as to
        provide a significantly safer crossing of Iliff Avenue for High Line Canal Trail users.
        As discussed under Historical Resources, a portion of the High Line Canal approximately one
        quarter of a mile north and south of Iliff Avenue was recommended as officially eligible for
        listing on the National Historic Register of Places, which was confirmed by the State Historic
        Preservation Office (CO Historical Society 2013).

Cherry Creek Greenway Trail
        The Cherry Creek Greenway Trail is a hard surface multi-use trail that runs parallel to Cherry
        Creek for over 40 miles from Confluence Park in
        downtown Denver to Castlewood Canyon State
        Park in Douglas County. Some segments of the
        trail include a "soft trail" parallel to the hard
        surface trail in the riparian zone. The trail is
        considered to be one of "the last remaining
        natural areas within the metropolitan area" and
        portions fall under the jurisdiction of Arapahoe
        County, City of Aurora, City of Centennial,
        Cherry Creek State Park (Colorado Parks and
        Wildlife), City and County of Denver, Douglas
        County, City of Glendale, Greenwood Village,
        and Town of Parker (Denver County 2012).


                                                    51
                                                                                                               Environmental Overview

           Trail users include pedestrians, cyclists, and equestrians. The Cherry Creek Trail runs along the
           west side of Cherry Creek north of Iliff Avenue and crosses to the east side of the creek via an
           underpass pedestrian bridge directly south of Iliff Avenue at Cherry Creek South Drive.
           The Cherry Creek Greenway Corridor Master Plan Report (2012) indicates that the future "vision"
           of the Cherry Creek Greenway corridor "promotes a high quality of life for local neighborhoods
           and improves the ability to safely travel within the community between residential, shopping
           and business locations, community facilities, social outlets, and other nearby destinations."

Cherry Creek Greenway
           Approximately 5.5 acres of land are designated as Cherry Creek Greenway along the Cherry
           Creek Trail south of Iliff Avenue. An additional 3.3 acres are designated as Wabash Trailhead.
           Both designated parklands are associated with the Cherry Creek Greenway Trail.

Next Steps
           During alternatives development, the conceptual design will consider means to avoid impacts to
           parks and recreational resources wherever possible. Once the alternatives being considered
           have been screened, potential Section 4(f) properties adjacent to the remaining alternatives
           may be evaluated for Section 4(f) Applicability. Avoidance and minimization measures
           considered during the alternatives evaluation will be documented as part of this corridor study
           process for use in the future Section 4(f) evaluation. Next steps for impacts to Section 4(f)
           resources during the NEPA process include a detailed analysis of the impacts of the project
           design to parkland and recreational resources and a Section 4(f) evaluation, which includes
           avoidance of park and recreational resources; mitigation, or measures to minimize harm;
           documentation of feasible and prudent avoidance alternatives; and coordination with FHWA
           and officials with jurisdiction.

Hazardous Materials
           This hazardous materials review provides information about properties within the study area
           that pose a potential risk of environmental contamination from hazardous materials. Sites with
           known (current and historic) soil and/or groundwater contamination are distinguished as sites
           with "recognized environmental conditions."3 A search of local, state, tribal, and federal
           environmental agency databases showed that a total of 189 sites with recognized
           environmental conditions were "flagged" within the study area (EDR 2013). A majority of these
           sites are located directly on or to the north of Iliff Avenue. Few sites are located to the south of
           Iliff Avenue (see Figure 25).
           Many of the identified sites were duplicate listings; once the duplicates were removed,
           approximately 112 unique sites remained. Site types include gas stations, above ground and
           underground storage tanks (USTs), asbestos abatement and demolition projects, dry cleaners,


3
  Recognized environmental, as defined conditions by the American Society for Testing and Materials Standard E 1527-05, include sites with
"the presence or likely presence of any hazardous substances or petroleum products on a property under conditions that indicate an existing
release, a past release, or a material threat of a release of any hazardous substances or petroleum products into structures on the property or
into the ground, groundwater, or surface water of the property" (ASTM Standard, 2005).
                                                                       52
                                                                                                               Environmental Overview

           poisons/toxins, and spills. A total of 24 sites were categorized as potential "high" impact
           locations; these sites are listed as Underground Storage Tanks, Leaking Underground Storage
           Tanks (LUST), LUST Trust, Above ground Tank, and/or for spillage. In addition, several of these
           sites are flagged for other potential impacts, such as Permitted Facility & Emissions Listing or
           methamphetamine lab locations (see Appendix D for a full list of these sites).
           The 88 remaining sites are considered low or medium potential impacts. While they are not
           considered high impact sites, there is potential for residual contamination to the soils in the
           study area.
           Where the potential for encountering contamination is suspected, avoidance or identification of
           potential mitigation measures can be implemented when reasonably possible. Environmental
           contaminants are most likely to be encountered during ground-disturbing activities at hazardous
           materials sites, and the contaminants at each site can be different. The most fundamental
           management for hazardous materials is to avoid contaminated sites, which often is not feasible.
           Wherever possible, responsibilities for known hazardous materials issues at properties targeted
           for construction should be resolved during acquisition. Additional assessment and/or field
           investigations may be required in future NEPA activities, right-of-way (ROW) acquisitions, or the
           development of specific materials management or controls required during construction.
           A hazardous materials assessment, such as a Modified Phase I Environmental Site Assessment,
           would typically be needed as part of future project development as part of the NEPA process. A
           more detailed hazardous material assessment would provide information to plan for known and
           potential hazardous materials and contaminated sites. During the final planning and design
           process, this information can be used to identify avoidance options, when possible, and to assist
           with the development of specific contaminated soils/groundwater material management or
           mitigation measures. Properties to be acquired may also require individual site assessments
           and/or preliminary site investigations as part of the ROW acquisition process, and may require
           remediation prior to acquisition or development.

Cultural
Cultural Resources (Historic
                   (Historic and Archeological Resources)
                                               Resources)
           Cultural resources such as archaeological, architectural, and historic interests can be affected by
           clearing, grading, excavation, and other construction activities and should be considered in the
           planning process relative to environmental and historic preservation laws. The National
           Historic Preservation Act requires federal agencies4 to take into account the effects of their
           actions on "historic properties." "Historic properties" are defined as properties that are included
           in the National Register of Historic Places (NRHP), which is the official list of America's historic
           places worthy of preservation, or that meet the criteria (i.e., are "eligible") for listing in the
           NRHP (Advisory Council on Historic Preservation 2002).
           If project development could affect cultural resources, the lead agency must identify the
           appropriate State Historic Preservation Officer (SHPO) to consult with during the process. The
           Colorado Historical Society/Office of Archeology and Historic Preservation (the SHPO for


4
  Although Colorado counties are not federal agencies, potential future federal funding for this project would qualify as involvement by a
federal agency.

           Colorado) performed a file search in March 2013 to identify known cultural resources in the
           study area. A historic overview of study area development has been included to support the
           evaluation of cultural resources. The study also provides better understanding of historical
           patterns, themes, and periods that may contribute to the significance of cultural resources.
           The file search for historic resources revealed that seven field surveys have been conducted
           within the study area between 1980 and 2011. A total of thirteen sites were identified and
           evaluated to determine if they meet eligibility criteria for the NHRP. Nine potentially eligible
           historic resources and one officially eligible resource were identified in the study area.
           Additionally, three sites were identified in the study area and determined to be officially not
           eligible: Huntington Estates, Butterfield ­ Overland Trail ­ Starvation Trail, and the Smoky Hill
           Trail- Middle Branch. No archaeological resources were identified. Figure 25 shows the locations

Next Steps
     All of the trails intersect the corridor. Three sites are located near the corridor and have the
     potential to be impacted. Historical and archaeological sites are not renewable; as such, the best
     resource management is to avoid impacts to properties listed or evaluated as eligible for
     inclusion on the NRHP. Avoiding or minimizing impacts to historic and archaeological resources
     can be accomplished by avoiding direct and indirect impacts to known NHRP-eligible or listed
     resources during alternative development and design. If impacts are unavoidable they should be
     mitigated by data recovery, analysis, and publication of findings.
     If federal funding is used to implement improvements, compliance with Section 106 of the
     National Historic Preservation Act is required. Section 106 requires federal agencies to consider
     the effects of their undertakings upon significant (NRHP-listed or eligible) historic properties.

Paleontological Resources
     Paleontology is the study of the ancient life; paleontological resources are the fossils that
     remain (NPS n.d.). The rocks of the Denver Basin (the area from Fort Collins to Colorado Springs
     to Limon) are rich with fossils. These rocks record the Cretaceous-Tertiary (KT) boundary, which
     marks the end of the dinosaurs, and is studied by scientists to understand how life and the earth
     changed at that time. Some of Colorado's most remarkable fossil sites have been found during
     road and building construction (Denver Museum of Nature and Science 2011). Therefore, fossils
     could be uncovered during project development.
     As a matter of Best Management Practices, any capital improvement should avoid any known
     fossil localities. Adverse impacts to paleontological resources can be mitigated to below the
     level of significance with the implementation of mitigation that would salvage fossils from
     construction excavations and permanently house them in a public museum. When working in a
     known fossiliferous geologic formation, it may be advantageous to have a paleontologist
     available during construction. If any fossils are exposed during construction, ground-disturbing
     work should be suspended until a state-certified Paleontologist can be notified to assess the
     fossil find. If the fossil find is significant, appropriate recovery and documentation actions may
     be necessary.

Air Quality
     Consideration of air quality impacts is important because the project is located in an area with a
     history of urban air pollution for which motor vehicle use has been a key emissions source.


                                                 56
                                                                               Environmental Overview

     The following pollutants are normally considered:
             Carbon monoxide ­ there has been no violation of the national ambient air quality
             standard (NAAQS) for this pollutant in the Denver region since 1995.
             Particulate matter 10 microns in diameter or smaller (PM10) ­ there has been no
             violation of the NAAQS for this pollutant in the Denver region since 1993.
             Particulate matter 2.5 microns in diameter or smaller (PM2.5) ­ there has never been a
             recorded violation of this NAAQS in the Denver region.
             Ozone ­ Due to continued violations of the 8-hour NAAQS for this pollutant, the Denver
             region has been classified as a marginal nonattainment area with a mandated deadline
             to meet the standard by 2015.
     Roadway improvements can have an impact on emissions and concentration of a class of
     potentially carcinogenic pollutants called Mobile Source Air Toxics, especially if large numbers of
     diesel vehicles are in use. No NAAQS levels have been established for these pollutants.
     Iliff Avenue between Quebec Street and Parker Road is a four-lane arterial street carrying about
     33,000 vehicles on an average weekday. For the approximate two-mile stretch, this equates to
     approximately 66,000 daily vehicle-miles of travel (VMT). As DRCOG estimated that total
     regional travel was 74 million VMT in the year 2010, this portion of Iliff Avenue accounts for less
     than 0.1% of the regional total. The DRCOG Long Range Transportation Plan projects that
     regional VMT will increase an estimated 60% by the year 2035. No such increase is expected to
     occur on Iliff Avenue based on the study travel demand forecasts. Hence, the negligible 0.1%
     VMT share of regional travel on Iliff Avenue will decline substantially in the future.
     Nearby land uses do not include major origins or destinations for truck freight. As an arterial
     street with a speed limit of 40 MPH, with side street access and stoplights, Iliff Avenue does not
     attract or carry a significant amount of heavy truck traffic. It also does not carry a notable
     amount of diesel bus traffic, serving only two bus routes.
     Based on all of the above conditions, air quality issues will be minimal for this roadway corridor
     and little or no analysis is expected to be required for the operation of the existing and
     improved configurations of this facility.

Construction Impacts
     Construction emissions were evaluated qualitatively. Construction emissions would be
     temporary and are not anticipated to cause air quality violations. Construction activities that
     could affect air quality include soil-disturbing activities, heavy-duty equipment exhaust,
     commuting workers' motor vehicle exhaust, and asphalt paving. The total emissions and the
     timing of the emissions from these sources would vary depending on the phasing of the
     construction activities, and would not all occur simultaneously. Construction-related emissions
     would be temporary and typically of short duration. Typical sources of emissions during
     construction of transportation projects include the following:
             Fugitive dust generated during excavation, grading, loading, and unloading activities.
             Dust generated during demolition of structures and pavement.

                                                 57
                                                                                 Environmental Overview

                Engine exhaust emissions from construction vehicles, worker vehicles, and diesel fuel-
                fired construction equipment.
                Volatile organic compounds and odorous compounds emitted during asphalt paving.
                Increased motor vehicle emissions associated with increased traffic congestion during
                construction.
        Contract documents for any construction project in the corridor should require the contractor(s)
        to comply with all applicable Colorado air quality regulations during construction of the project.
        Site-specific mitigation methods will be determined during construction.

Noise
        Noise is typically defined as unwanted or undesirable sound, where sound is characterized by
        small air pressure fluctuations above and below the atmospheric pressure. The basic three
        parameters of environmental noise that affect human subjective response are: (1) intensity or
        level, (2) frequency content, and (3) variation with time. The first parameter is determined by
        how greatly the sound pressure fluctuates above and below the atmospheric pressure and is
        expressed on a scale in units of decibels. Using this scale, the range of normally encountered
        sound can be expressed by values between zero and 120 decibels. On a relative basis, a 3-
        decibel change in sound level generally represents a barely noticeable change outside the
        laboratory, whereas a 10-decibel change in sound level would typically be perceived as a
        doubling (or halving) of the loudness of a sound.
        The frequency content of noise is related to the tone or pitch of the sound, and is expressed
        based on the rate of the air pressure fluctuation in terms of cycles per second (called Hertz). The
        human ear can detect a wide range of frequencies from about 20 Hertz to 17,000 Hertz.
        However, because the sensitivity of human hearing varies with frequency, the A-weighting
        system is commonly used when measuring environmental noise. This system provides a single
        number descriptor that correlates with human subjective response. "A-weighted" sound levels
        are expressed in decibel notation as "dBA." The A-weighted sound level is widely accepted by
        acousticians as a proper unit for describing environmental noise.
        Most commonly encountered outdoor noise sources generate sound levels within the range of
        60 dBA to 90 dBA at a distance of 50 feet. Because environmental noise fluctuates from moment
        to moment, it is common practice to condense all of this information into a single number,
        called the "equivalent" sound level (Leq). Leq can be thought of as the steady sound level that
        represents the same sound energy as the varying sound levels over a specified time period. For
        roadway projects this is typically 1 hour.

Regulations
        Arapahoe County does not have applicable traffic noise ordinances or policies. If future
        improvements involve the use of state and federal funds, the project would be subject to both
        Federal regulations and State noise guidelines.




                                                    58
                                                                                  Environmental Overview

Noise Prediction Modeling
        Noise levels were predicted using the TNM 2.5 highway noise level prediction software program
        (TNM)developed by the Federal Highway Administration, which is approved for use on CDOT
        and Federal-aid projects. TNM calculates the hourly, A-weighted Leq at a receptor location given
        the noise emission level of automobiles, medium and heavy trucks, buses and motorcycles, the
        volume and speed of each of these vehicle types on each key roadway, the relative location of
        all roadways, receptors, and terrain features, and the type of land cover between each receptor
        and each roadway. Data collection and model validation were in accordance with standard
        practices prescribed by the Federal Highway Administration.
        The roadway alignments, including profiles and pavement width, for Iliff Avenue were
        determined using the topographical data developed for the project. Existing and future (2035)
        traffic projections for this project were based on AM and PM traffic counts, including vehicle
        classification. These existing and predicted 2035 peak hour traffic volumes and vehicle types
        were used to develop the noise models. Due to the preliminary nature of the project scope, no
        effort was made to identify receptor locations behind fences and walls. Receptors were located
        in unobstructed areas in close proximity to residential areas. The exact locations and elevations
        of the representative receptors were determined using the topographic files. In the TNM
        model, five feet were added to the elevation of each receiver to account for the height of a
        typical person's ear. Terrain features such as field grass, existing structures and fences can
        affect the way noise travels. Where appropriate, those features were included in the model.

Field Measurements and Model Validation
        The model data were validated by measuring noise levels at four locations in the field and
        comparing the measured values to the TMN model results. Field noise measurements were
        conducted, using a Quest 2900 integrating/logging level Type II meter, on Monday, May 13,
        2013. This day was warm and dry, with predominantly low wind speeds (i.e., less than 10 mph).
        Two measurements were taken at four different sites within the project area. Intermittent gusts
        invalidated some readings and these readings were not retained. Each measurement was sited
        within 100 of the roadway edge. During the measurement, the numbers of automobiles,
        medium trucks, heavy trucks, buses and motorcycles on Iliff Avenue and adjacent facilities were
        documented. Average speeds were determined by driving in the traffic flow.
        Using the inputs noted above, noise levels were predicted at each field measurement location.
        The model is considered validated and is accurately predicting the existing noise environment
        when the model predictions are within ± 3 dBA of the field measured noise levels.

Predicted Noise Level
        The validated noise models were the basis for the development of the existing and future year
        (2035) noise prediction models. The results of the existing and future year (2035) traffic noise
        models indicate that, in unobstructed areas of the corridor, existing traffic noise could reach 65
        to 66 dBA, standard levels for conversational speech, at 100 feet from the edge of the Iliff
        Avenue through travel lanes as shown in Figure 26. Based on the fully developed nature of the
        corridor and the limited capacity for significantly greater traffic volumes in the future, the 2035
        noise levels are imperceptibly, less than 3 dBA louder than current levels.

Environmental Justice
           Environmental Justice (EJ) is the fair treatment of all races, cultures, and incomes with respect to
           the development, adoption, implementation, and enforcement of environmental laws and
           policies, as defined by Federal law. EJ originates from Title VI of the Civil Rights Act of 1964,
           which states that "No person in the US shall, on the ground of race, color, or national origin be
           excluded from participation in, be denied the benefits or, or be subjected to discrimination
           under any program or activity receiving federal financial assistance" (CDOT 2013). Potential
           adverse impacts to minority and low-income populations could result from:
                      Property loss due to right-of-way acquisition
                      A change in air quality and noise impacts
                      Destruction or diminution of aesthetic values
                      Destruction or disruption of community cohesion or a community's economic vitality
                      Destruction or disruption of the availability of public and private facilities
           Arapahoe County has adopted public involvement policies that require special outreach to
           specific federally distinguished groups, including minorities and low-income persons. Several
           neighborhoods with concentrations of minorities and low-income persons that are considered
           to be EJ target areas exist within the study area, as shown in Figure 27 and Figure 28.
           Low-income is defined as a household income at or below the Department of Human Services'
           poverty guidelines (CDOT 1997), which are based on the Census Bureau poverty thresholds.
           2007 ­ 2011 American Community Survey Census tract data6 was used to identify that
           communities within the study area south of Iliff Avenue and east of Parker Road have a higher
           percentage of low-income residents (see Census tract 69.01, 873, and 68.13).
           The second evaluation of EJ populations is for minority populations. These thresholds are
           determined by calculating the percentage of the population per Census block group that is
           greater than the County average using 2010 Census tract data. Within the study area, a total of
           five census block groups are considered with a higher percentage of minority populations. These
           block groups (872-1, 872-3, 873-1) are concentrated around the west end of the study area as
           well as a portion east of Parker Road (block group 871-2 and a sliver of block group 68. 13-1).
           The following steps are recommended to minimize and mitigate impacts to EJ populations.
                      Evaluate potential impacts to EJ populations during the alternatives evaluation for use in
                      any future NEPA evaluations.
                      Document feasible and prudent avoidance and/or mitigation measures during the
                      alternatives evaluation and design phases.
                      Coordinate with FHWA and other officials with jurisdiction.


6
 Census tracts are small, relatively permanent statistical subdivisions of a county. Within the Census tracts are Census block groups, which are
the smallest geographic area for which the Bureau of the Census collects decennial census data. Census tracts are evaluated for low-income
populations while minority populations are determined by the block groups.
